<div id="review_4419356" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4419356" />
    <a title="Jacob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/271503-jacob"><img alt="Jacob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186967432p2/271503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4419356?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob" href="/user/show/271503-jacob">Jacob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271503-jacob?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer4419356&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4419356" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2480526087094285598">When he was unable to find a publisher for this, his second novel, Flann O'Brien famously stashed the manuscript away in a drawer and told his friends that it had been lost. Some commentators actually think that he was <i>scared</i> of what he had been written, that something about it upset his meek Catholic soul. I don't know about that, but the book's undoubtedly disquieting. The novel sometimes feels like the clanking boiler room behind the scenes of another, less nonsensical novel, and takes place </span>
  <span id="freeText2480526087094285598" style="display:none">When he was unable to find a publisher for this, his second novel, Flann O'Brien famously stashed the manuscript away in a drawer and told his friends that it had been lost. Some commentators actually think that he was <i>scared</i> of what he had been written, that something about it upset his meek Catholic soul. I don't know about that, but the book's undoubtedly disquieting. The novel sometimes feels like the clanking boiler room behind the scenes of another, less nonsensical novel, and takes place in what we are given to believe is an eternally recurring afterlife, where no greater intelligence or divinity is present, or if one is present, it's been shattered into traces that cannot be coherently reassembled -- infinity, authority, punishment. As a young man, O'Brien had been much influenced by James Joyce, and his first novel drew many comparisons to <i>Ulysses</i>, enough to irritate the author for the rest of his life. <i>The Third Policeman</i> takes many of the qualities of Joyce's great book and inverts or reduces them. <i>Ulysses</i>, a humane and generous novel, invites an infinite number of possibilities and interpretations; <i>The Third Policeman</i> is a narrative closed loop and resists explication.<br /><br />The book is also very, very funny. It's one of the very best modern novels, and, sadly, because it was written very early in his career, the finest thing Flann O'Brien ever wrote.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2480526087094285598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3475586240" />
    <a title="Katia N" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7381993-katia-n"><img alt="Katia N" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370960553p2/7381993.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3475586240?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katia N" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katia N" href="/user/show/7381993-katia-n">Katia N</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3475586240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11617908497267168756">Using the words from the novel it "is unbearable breathless yellow day" here. It is too hot for me to write a proper review. And, I think it is the one of those novels that should be read at least twice. I could only say for now that I would not be able to look at a bicycle for a while without thinking that it might be alive:-) I would like the book even more if I would not have read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38787.Pedro_P_ramo' title='Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pedro Páramo</a> or would not be familiar the Russian absurdist tradition from Gogol to Kharms and even Bulgakov. Ped</span>
  <span id="freeText11617908497267168756" style="display:none">Using the words from the novel it "is unbearable breathless yellow day" here. It is too hot for me to write a proper review. And, I think it is the one of those novels that should be read at least twice. I could only say for now that I would not be able to look at a bicycle for a while without thinking that it might be alive:-) I would like the book even more if I would not have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38787.Pedro_P_ramo" title="Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo" rel="nofollow noopener">Pedro Páramo</a> or would not be familiar the Russian absurdist tradition from Gogol to Kharms and even Bulgakov. Pedro Paramo addresses the similar theme but in a rather different way, the absurdists - the style and humour, to some extent.<br /><br />My favourite quote: <br /><br /><i>"Down into the earth where dead men go I would go soon and maybe come out of it again in some healthy way, free and innocent of all human perplexity. I would perhaps be the chill of an April wind, an essential part of some indomitable river or be personally concerned in the ageless perfection of some rank mountain bearing down upon the mind by occupying forever a position in the blue easy distance. Or perhaps a smaller thing like movement in the grass on an unbearable breathless yellow day, some hidden creature going about its business - I might well be responsible for that or for some part of it. Or even those unaccountable distinctions that make an evening recognisable from its own morning, the smells and sounds and sights of the perfected and matured essences of the day, these might not be innocent of my meddling and my abiding presence." </i></span>

<div id="review_365305066" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/365305066" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54697-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/365305066?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/54697-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007247172" href="/book/show/587369.The_Third_Policeman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=eire">eire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=underrated-lost-classics">underrated-lost-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=fictions-of-the-big-it">fictions-of-the-big-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer365305066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2206281315499258198"><br/>50 pages in thus far, read it on the flight back from Ireland. <br/><br/>Amazing so far, real unique and mindbending and subtly lyrical. ASTB was as grand as all that, this is supposed to be (possibly) even better. Here's hoping the brilliant start keeps up...<br/><br/>***<br/><br/> Now I'm at pg.87 (significant for a barely over 200 page tome) and it's still sneakily, uniquely, obliquely brilliant. <br/><br/> Cool kids know that Flann O'Brien is one of the finest underrated (Irish) writers of the 20th Century and that At Swim-Two-Bir</span>
  <span id="freeText2206281315499258198" style="display:none"><br />50 pages in thus far, read it on the flight back from Ireland. <br /><br />Amazing so far, real unique and mindbending and subtly lyrical. ASTB was as grand as all that, this is supposed to be (possibly) even better. Here's hoping the brilliant start keeps up...<br /><br />***<br /><br /> Now I'm at pg.87 (significant for a barely over 200 page tome) and it's still sneakily, uniquely, obliquely brilliant. <br /><br /> Cool kids know that Flann O'Brien is one of the finest underrated (Irish) writers of the 20th Century and that At Swim-Two-Birds is a bumptious, multifaceted, meta-masterpiece (by which I mean both deeply and indistinguishably metafictional and a masterpeice which likes to subvert its own masterful language at every turn, making it more punk rock than a stately, arch-Modernist string quartet). <br /><br /> One writer put it finely when he opined to the extent that (at least in terms of 20th Century Irish fiction, and let's face it, Ireland has a fine claim on some of the best that was thought and said amid the caterwaul 20th Century) as far as Catholic heirarchies go, Joyce is the father, Beckett is the son, and O'Brien is the holy ghost. That seems about right to me. Compared to the emblematically-maximalist Joyce and the ur-minimalist Beckett, O'Brien is the antic spirit amid the ruins of the textual world. <br /><br /> Instead of grounding his texts in style and linguistic virtuosity, a la the aforementioned (who, it's worth adding, were early and vocal supporters of his work) O'Brien shakes the ontological foundations of his world consistently and playfully, opening up new, rather unheard-of narrative spaces in the process. <br /><br /> His relationship to Joyce during his own time was ambivalent at best; alternately repectful, familiarly irreverent, and agog with annoyance and lazy oversaturation but I think he got a little too caught up in the unneccesary anxiety of influence to be rightfully greivous at being subsumed in Joyce's infinite labyrinth. <br /><br /> O'Brien is and was his own man, and this is an extremely good thing not only for his reputation but also for the standards of quality and innovation for 20th Century letters. <br /><br /> Joyce can be fairly said to have mastered the Modernism of his era, which is all to the good, but O'Brien not only anticipates but diagnoses and revels in the post-modernity to come. All that had been solidly made (and that's a capital-M, ifyoudon'tmind, artist: god of the creation, remember) or fashioned melts into air, and O'Brien (Brian O'Nolan to his mother and the civil service; "O'Brien" rivals Kierkegaard in terms of mastery of the <i> nom de plume </i>) is the wind chime.<br /><br /> This much is true, as well: the EVEN COOLER kids know that The Third Policeman is even better than A-S-T-B. I'm not (overly) fond of literary one-upsmanship but I like to think I know a fine thing when I see it and this text is amazing. Consistently, wittily, deeply and darkly amazing.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2206281315499258198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8810204343208252266">Bizarrely good. An aura of strangeness tinged the first few pages, and then it intensified, and then there was a surreal tumble down the rabbit hole into a very curious world. A place where "...the trees were active where they stood." You need to "use your internal imagination". <br/>Descriptions and events and expounded philosophies sort of made a weak and tenuous sense. The edge of sense. Until you realise it was making no sense at all and you were lost again. But then another promising thread of l</span>
  <span id="freeText8810204343208252266" style="display:none">Bizarrely good. An aura of strangeness tinged the first few pages, and then it intensified, and then there was a surreal tumble down the rabbit hole into a very curious world. A place where "...the trees were active where they stood." You need to "use your internal imagination". <br />Descriptions and events and expounded philosophies sort of made a weak and tenuous sense. The edge of sense. Until you realise it was making no sense at all and you were lost again. But then another promising thread of logic is offered and eagerly grasped. It only takes you deeper.<br /> <br />Some of the incidental descriptions of the land, the surroundings, were beautiful. "The dawn was contagious, spreading rapidly about the heavens. Birds were stirring and the great kingly trees were being pleasingly interfered with by the first breezes."<br />"The road...ran away westwards in the mist of the early morning, running cunningly through the little hills and going to some trouble to visit tiny towns which were not, strictly speaking, on its way."<br />Time and space are interchangeable. "... he led the way heavily into the middle of the morning." This is not logical, yet it makes sense.<br />Inanimate things don't become animated but they do assume a different essence, all explainable by the Atomic Theory. Which again made a weird sort of illogical sense. <br />"...you would know how certain the sureness of certainty is"<br /><br />A delightful, bendy-mind kind of book that will take your brain out for a run, and then won't return it to the same spot.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1505797350" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1505797350" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1505797350?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780452259126" href="/book/show/890858.The_Third_Policeman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=oneirics">oneirics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1505797350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16025752282997999553">It would be easy to dismiss bits of this as sheer exercise in absurdism, which O'Brien's stature as a comic writer might tend to suggest. But there's also his relation to Joyce in Irish literature, the feeling that O'Brien writes science fiction with the imagination of a more erudite Philip K. Dick, the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/507725.Locus_Solus' title='Locus Solus by Raymond Roussel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Locus Solus</a>-like wonder and bafflement, and an almost Lovecraftian sense of the obscurely ominous. Really it's this incredible terror or the infinite and unknowable (which actually has a perfect </span>
  <span id="freeText16025752282997999553" style="display:none">It would be easy to dismiss bits of this as sheer exercise in absurdism, which O'Brien's stature as a comic writer might tend to suggest. But there's also his relation to Joyce in Irish literature, the feeling that O'Brien writes science fiction with the imagination of a more erudite Philip K. Dick, the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/507725.Locus_Solus" title="Locus Solus by Raymond Roussel" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus Solus</a>-like wonder and bafflement, and an almost Lovecraftian sense of the obscurely ominous. Really it's this incredible terror or the infinite and unknowable (which actually has a perfect place within the plot) -- the humor always turns on a disconcerting edge, and there's the creeping suspicion that our worst fears about the uncertain world are (<a>as Aubrey brilliantly point out</a>) actually being bourn out by scientific reality forming in the interwar period of its conception. Because almost as much as this is about science fiction (or about the subconscious, or nightmares, both of which very much have their place here) this is equally about science itself -- the latest theories, empirical measurement, the experiments of the mad or misguided which may point the way to discovery. It's even a parodic scientific text (complete with footnotes that must have been among the most hilariously excessive of its pre-DFW times). Much as I've known I had to read O'Brien eventually, this blows away my expectations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16025752282997999553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_991645338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/991645338" />
    <a title="mwpm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm"><img alt="mwpm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516943741p2/13827810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/991645338?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mwpm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mwpm" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm">mwpm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586087497" href="/book/show/890856.The_Third_Policeman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827810-mwpm?shelf=novels-novellas">novels-novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer991645338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5064464777047867654">Anyone who has read and enjoyed <i>The Third Policeman</i> will be devastated to learn of its initial rejection, and that after its rejection the author claimed the manuscript was "lost", and that it would have remained "lost" had it not been discovered after the author's death and published posthumously.<br/><br/>I, for one, was devastated... devastated, but not surprised - given the novel's outlandish story, falling somewhere on the border between the Surreal and Absurd. <br/><br/>Indeed, <i>The Third Policeman</i> was written</span>
  <span id="freeText5064464777047867654" style="display:none">Anyone who has read and enjoyed <i>The Third Policeman</i> will be devastated to learn of its initial rejection, and that after its rejection the author claimed the manuscript was "lost", and that it would have remained "lost" had it not been discovered after the author's death and published posthumously.<br /><br />I, for one, was devastated... devastated, but not surprised - given the novel's outlandish story, falling somewhere on the border between the Surreal and Absurd. <br /><br />Indeed, <i>The Third Policeman</i> was written after the Surrealist movement of the 1920s and before the publication of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/957894.Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">Albert Camus</a>'s <i>Myth of Sisyphus</i> and the subsequent rise of Absurdist writers (including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett" rel="nofollow noopener">Samuel Beckett</a>, who expressed an affinity for Flann O'Brien).<br /><br />The novel's only discrepancy is the conflicting relationship between form and content. Following the literary trends of the time, the style is somewhat dense and descriptive ("descriptive" by my standards). Whereas the content ranges from surreal to absurd, from silly to ridiculous, from nonsensical to outlandish - in the best way possible, of course.<br /><br />From the first line of the novel ("Not everybody knows how I killed old Philip Mathers...") the tone of the novel is unclear. One might suspect (as much from the first line as from the novel's title) that this is a crime novel or police procedural of some sort. This suspicion is quickly eliminated. Just as abruptly as the narrator has announced the murder of Philip Mathers, he changes the subject to De Selby, a theorist that the author holds in such high regard that he's inspired to write an Index. The narrator's interest in De Selby is evident as he frequently references the theorist and even includes extensive footnotes throughout the novel.<br /><br />In fact, De Selby is not a real theorist but a character invented by the author, much like Kurt Vonnegut's Kilgore Trout. And, like Trout in the novels of Vonnegut, De Selby makes an apparence in another of O'Brien's novels, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59642.The_Dalkey_Archive" title="The Dalkey Archive by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dalkey Archive</a>
</i> (which also happens to be the name of the publisher responsible for reprinting O'Brien's various works).<br /><br />By the second chapter, the texture of novel has changed completely. The narrator is no longer grounded in the real world. He has entered a surreal new world, populated by surreal characters - including Philip Mathers (the man he murdered in the first line of the novel); a one-legged man (with whom the one-legged man narrator establishes a sort of solidarity); and three policemen...<br /><br />The first, Sergeant Puck, is preoccupied with bicycles. He believes that every crime is somehow related to bicycles. (He greets anyone who enters the station with: "Is it about a bicycle?") The narrator devises a plan to extract some information from the policemen by reporting to have lost a golden watch. The Sergeant responds to the narrator's report with disbelief; he cannot comprehend why someone would steal a golden watch - after all, you cannot ride a golden watch. His belief can be summarized thus...<br /><br />"'There was a man in this room a fortnight ago,' he said, 'telling me that he was at the loss of his mother, a lady of eight-two. When I asked him for a description - just to fill up the blanks in the official form we get for half-nothing from the Stationery Office - he said she had rust on her rims and that her back brakes were subject to the jerks.'" (pg. 65)<br /><br />The second, Policeman MacCruiskeen, is preoccupied with the construction of a chest, along with incrementally smaller chests contained within the chest (like Russian Dolls). The decision to construct smaller chests, MacCruiskeen explains, arose from the question of what of what to contain within the chest, concluding 'that the only sole correct thing to contain in the chest was another chest of the same make but littler in cubic dimension.' (pg. 73) His logic reaches its comedic pinnacle when he describes constructing a chest so small that it took 'three years to make and (...) another year to believe that I had made it.' (pg. 75)<br /><br />And the third, Policeman Fox... Suffice to say that he is shrouded in mystery, much in the same way as the third man from Graham Greene's <i>The Third Man</i>, or the third murderer from <i>Macbeth</i>. (It is worth mentioning that O'Brien's first novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a>
</i>, was endorsed by Greene - who was, at that time, a reader at Longman Publishers.)<br /><br />The logic of the novel is best exemplified by an incident recalled by the Sergeant...<br /><br /><blockquote><i>'I heard of a man once,' he said, 'that had himself let up into the sky in a balloon to make observations, a man of great personal charm but a divil for reading books. They played out the rope till he was disappeared completely from all appearences, telescopes or no telescopes, and then they played out another ten miles of rope to make sure of first-class observations. When the time-limit for the observations was over they pulled down the balloon again and lo and behold there was no man in the basket and his dead body was never found afterwards lying dead or alive in any parish ever afterwards.'<br />(...)<br />'But they were clever enough to think of sending up the balloon again a fortnight later and when they brought it down the second time lo and behold the man was sitting in the basket without a feather out of him if any of my information can be believed at all.'</i> (pg. 163-164)</blockquote><br /><br />It is important to note that the policemen are unreliable narrators, their speech ruled by non sequitur and their logic dictated by nonsense. That the Sergeant makes a point of saying "if any of my information can be believed at all" is a testament to the ambiguity and uncertainty that surrounds the policemen, everything they say and do, and the world they inhabit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5064464777047867654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_87295979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87295979" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87295979?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586087497" href="/book/show/890856.The_Third_Policeman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pogue-mahone">pogue-mahone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=dalkey-by-proxy">dalkey-by-proxy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87295979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15802075444817248608"><i>Note: This review was written on September 9th 2007. I was young and extremely ill-read, so indulgence is required.</i><br/><br/><b>A Footnote to Genius</b><br/><br/>In the presence of literary giants, it can be impossible to hold one’s own. Often this is because most of them are long gone, and few would have the time for piffling fools such as me anyway, brandishing their flaccid members and asking for a furtive chug. Forgive the vulgar start. See, a few months back I locked myself in a room with <i>The Third Policeman</i> and crum</span>
  <span id="freeText15802075444817248608" style="display:none"><i>Note: This review was written on September 9th 2007. I was young and extremely ill-read, so indulgence is required.</i><br /><br /><b>A Footnote to Genius</b><br /><br />In the presence of literary giants, it can be impossible to hold one’s own. Often this is because most of them are long gone, and few would have the time for piffling fools such as me anyway, brandishing their flaccid members and asking for a furtive chug. Forgive the vulgar start. See, a few months back I locked myself in a room with <i>The Third Policeman</i> and crumbled under the weight of its erudition, subtlety and genre-bending humour. The experience stultified me.<br /><br />I have no idea what this book is about. Flann O’Brien has managed to baffle readers for over half a century with this schizophrenic masterwork, least of all the original publisher who refused to let it see the light 27 years after its composition. One might have thought having Samuel Beckett and James Joyce as converted fans would be something of an advantage, but on this occasion it seemed insufficient. Perhaps it stultified them too.<br /><br />As a lifelong member of the Civil Service, Flann O’Brien was an active writer for the Irish Times and known for his Cruiskeen Lawn column for over three decades, his trademark being the pinnacle of Irish satire and his perfect grasp over the comedy of the absurd. When viewed as one particular type of fiction, this book falls into obscurity and impossibility since it is untamed by nature, embracing the loose absurdity of burgeoning postmodernist playwrights and indeed fellow Irish contemporaries such as Joyce.<br /><br />The novel begins with the detached narrator confessing a murder. From this point onwards, things start to bend, buckle and twist. Events are explained with a deviatory logic, often written in a scientific perspective which helps to bring alive the crucial streak of surreal humour which binds the loose series of interconnected chapters. The narrator goes onto discuss his personal fascination with a scientist named De Selby, at which point this mythical figure takes hold of the narrative to a frustrating degree and my own mind started to ache.<br /><br />The narrator discusses houses and roads with a scholar’s attention-to-detail before the topic of bicycles is introduced; seemingly crucial for a basic understanding of the book (which I am inching towards). <i>The Third Policeman</i> moves from the murder theme onto a large treatise on bicycles, replete with ludicrous annotations which subvert the layout of the novel as it existed then, used for (overextended) comic effect. The text is more a series of vignettes unless the reader makes a note of all the bending Flann O’Brien conducts throughout his narrative and there is a lot of bending. Oodles of it. Vicarious pleasure can be found in the picturesque sense of the small Irish village he creates and the resonations here are apparent throughout all manner of contemporary Irish literature.<br /><br />I believe this novel holds weight more as an exercise in literary invention rather than being “about anything” in particular. As the blurb indicates, it is a dictionary of the absurd, successful through its unconditional surrender to the rules of nothing except sheer chaos. Perhaps there is nothing to understand about this novel. Perhaps O’Brien just wishes to bend people’s mind until they burst like balloon animals. Perhaps the point is that there is no point. Perhaps I am an idiot. All are possible, except the latter which is a given. There is no real plot to follow and the reader is forced to walk the tightrope between superfluous stream of humorous deviations and facts next to the elegant first-person narrative of whatever is going on.<br /><br />On balance, this novel blends the twisting nightmare of <i>The Life &amp; Opinions of Tristram Shandy, Gentleman</i> with the parochial density of <i>Finnegans Wake</i> and sets it against the backdrop of gleeful nonsense found in <i>Alice In Wonderland</i>. A combination of these works is in itself a massive achievement and the most important thing to relish is that <i>The Third Policeman</i> is much funnier than all of these classics, making it a desirable catch-all for those who wish to slip into the random madness of a hilarious literary genius.<br /><br />So I recommend four or five of you open-minded readers pick this up and get gloriously confused. Then get together and argue about what it all meant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15802075444817248608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer150033246516892539">I really wanted to like this book. I didn't like this book.<br/><br/>Things I did like:<br/><br/>* The writing. O'Brien was a wordsmith. In the abstract, all of his sentences please, and some astound.<br/><br/>* Bicycle sex. I'm not positive, but I'm pretty sure that the narrator got it on with a bicycle.   <span id="freeText150033246516892539" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this book. I didn't like this book.<br /><br />Things I did like:<br /><br />* The writing. O'Brien was a wordsmith. In the abstract, all of his sentences please, and some astound.<br /><br />* Bicycle sex. I'm not positive, but I'm pretty sure that the narrator got it on with a bicycle. The bicycle was the aggressor.<br /><br />* IEDs! This is before there were IEDs, mind you.<br /><br />Things I didn't like:<br /><br />* How horrible the book was.<br /><br />* How long it took me to work my way through the book.<br /><br />* The number of times I wished I was reading a different book.<br /><br />In sum, save yourself the trouble and read Bicycle Sex: The Definitive Anthology instead. O'Brien was probably an amusing conversationalist after tweleve whiskeys, but this novel is about as amusing as the swine flu.</span>

<div id="review_1256250199" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1256250199" />
    <a title="Lee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6361389-lee"><img alt="Lee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598822566p2/6361389.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1256250199?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee" href="/user/show/6361389-lee">Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1256250199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7339890133921263659">Inspired, hilarious lunacy with a savage edge, and absolutely and unmistakably unique.<br/><br/><br/><br/>‘The first beginnings of wisdom,’ he said, ‘is to ask questions but never to answer any. You get wisdom from asking and I from not answering. Would you believe that there is a great increase in crime in this locality? Last year we had sixty-nine cases of no lights and four stolen. This year we have eighty-two cases of no lights, thirteen cases of riding on the footpath and four stolen. There was one case of wan</span>
  <span id="freeText7339890133921263659" style="display:none">Inspired, hilarious lunacy with a savage edge, and absolutely and unmistakably unique.<br /><br /><br /><br />‘The first beginnings of wisdom,’ he said, ‘is to ask questions but never to answer any. You get wisdom from asking and I from not answering. Would you believe that there is a great increase in crime in this locality? Last year we had sixty-nine cases of no lights and four stolen. This year we have eighty-two cases of no lights, thirteen cases of riding on the footpath and four stolen. There was one case of wanton damage to a three-speed gear, there is sure to be a claim at the next Court and the area of charge will be the parish. Before the year is out there is certain to be a pump stolen, a very depraved and despicable manifestation of criminality and a blot on the county.’ <br /><br />‘Indeed,’ I said.<br /></span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2838425139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11352921464037526987">The craic runs strong with this one. At times I was exhausted by the relentless absurd whimsicality, until I embraced the fact that all there is is relentless absurd whimsicality, and in doing so it started becoming quite a bit profound. I did keep checking my pockets to see if I'd been pilfered by a hidden hand while being told a tall tale- relentlessly tall. Flawless prose, really enjoyable and creative prose more importantly. And in general just a real last hurrah for imagination and style</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jz0XGQZ/Trjrg/HOXB3uLS3E9STnhv4fyFK251vcIt7IOUJxYmuBz/S9OGKZq3CpPn+4aLfmCWQCj3javG2Z6Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7HtHXtyFg2y5lPQMBB5ajlDanKY1iP3U/iNTCzEIx+KrfxI2uJFMG6aqPaDBqMQKQ2HR6mXoCq80/p021rl81Q==')})
  }
//]]>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_12279'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12279.Molloy_Malone_Dies_The_Unnamable?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Molloy, Malone Dies, The Unnamable (The Trilogy, #1-3)<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Samuel Beckett<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.28 avg rating &mdash; 8,138 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1958\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11068147338289121619\">The first novel of Samuel Beckett\'s mordant and exhilirating midcentury trilogy intoduces us to Molloy, who has been mysteriously incarcerated, and who subsequently escapes to go discover the whereabouts of his mother. In the latter part of this curi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11068147338289121619\" style=\"display:none\">The first novel of Samuel Beckett\'s mordant and exhilirating midcentury trilogy intoduces us to Molloy, who has been mysteriously incarcerated, and who subsequently escapes to go discover the whereabouts of his mother. In the latter part of this curious masterwork, a certain Jacques Moran is deputized by anonymous authorities to search for the aforementioned Molloy. In the trilogy\'s second novel, Malone, who might or might not be Molloy himself, addresses us with his ruminations while in the act of dying. The third novel consists of the fragmented monologue - delivered, like the monologues of the previous novels, in a mournful rhetoric that possesses the utmost splendor and beauty - of what might or might not an armless and legless creature living in an urn outside an eating house. Taken together, these three novels represent the high-water mark of the literary movement we call Modernism. Within their linguistic terrain, where stories are taken up, broken off, and taken up again, where voices rise and crumble and are resurrected, we can discern the essential lineaments of our modern condition, and encounter an awesome vision, tragic yet always compelling and always mysteriously invigorating, of consciousness trapped and struggling inside the boundaries of nature.<\/span>\n  <a Rami Elhanan is Israeli. They inhabit a world of conflict that colors every aspect of their daily lives, from the roads they are allowed to drive on, to the schools their daughters, Abir and Smadar, each attend, to the checkpoints, both physical and emotional, they must negotiate.\n\nTheir worlds shift irreparably after ten-year-old Abir is killed by a rubber bullet and thirteen-year-old Smadar becomes the victim of suicide bombers. When Bassam and Rami learn of each other\'s stories, they recognize the loss that connects them and they attempt to use their grief as a weapon for peace.\n\nMcCann crafts Apeirogon out of a universe of fictional and nonfictional material. He crosses centuries and continents, stitching together time, art, history, nature, and politics in a tale both heartbreaking and hopeful.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4750627803380301205">Pseudonym of <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15061436.Brian___Nuall_in' title='Brian Ó Nualláin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brian Ó Nualláin</a>
</b>, also known as Brian O'Nolan.<br/><br/>His English novels appeared under the name of Flann O’Brien, while his great Irish novel and his newspaper column (which appeared from 1940 to 1966) were signed <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/757952.Myles_na_gCopaleen' title='Myles na gCopaleen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Myles na gCopaleen</a>
</b> or <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13513965.Myles_na_Gopaleen' title='Myles na Gopaleen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Myles na Gopaleen</a>
</b> – the second being a phonetic rendering of the first. One of twelve brothers and sisters, he was born in 1911 in Strabane, Count</span>
  <span id="freeText4750627803380301205" style="display:none">Pseudonym of <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15061436.Brian___Nuall_in" title="Brian Ó Nualláin" rel="nofollow noopener">Brian Ó Nualláin</a>
</b>, also known as Brian O'Nolan.<br /><br />His English novels appeared under the name of Flann O’Brien, while his great Irish novel and his newspaper column (which appeared from 1940 to 1966) were signed <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/757952.Myles_na_gCopaleen" title="Myles na gCopaleen" rel="nofollow noopener">Myles na gCopaleen</a>
</b> or <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13513965.Myles_na_Gopaleen" title="Myles na Gopaleen" rel="nofollow noopener">Myles na Gopaleen</a>
</b> – the second being a phonetic rendering of the first. One of twelve brothers and sisters, he was born in 1911 in Strabane, County Tyrone, into an Irish-speaking family. His father had learned Irish while a young man during the Gaelic revival the son was later to mock. O’Brien’s childhood has been described as happy, though somewhat insular, as the language spoken at home was not that spoken by their neighbours. The Irish language had long been in decline, and Strabane was not in an Irish-speaking part of the country. The family moved frequently during O’Brien’s childhood, finally settling in Dublin in 1925. Four years later O’Brien took up study in University College Dublin.<br /><br />Flann O'Brien is considered a major figure in twentieth century Irish literature. Flann O'Brien novels have attracted a wide following for their bizarre humour and Modernist metafiction.<br /><br />The café and shop of Cultúrlann McAdam Ó Fiaich (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.culturlann.ie">www.culturlann.ie</a>), at the heart of the Belfast Gaeltacht Quarter, is named <i>An Ceathrú Póilí</i> ("The Fourth Policeman"), as a play-on-words of the title of O'Brien's book <i>The Third Policeman</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4750627803380301205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You mean that because I have no name I cannot die and that you cannot be held answerable for death even if you kill me?"<br /><br />"That is about the size of it," said the Sergeant.<br /><br />I felt so sad and so entirely disappointed that tears came into my eyes and a lump of incommunicable poignancy swelled tragically in my throat. I began to feel intensely every fragment of my equal humanity. The life that was bubbling at the end of my fingers was real and nearly painful in intensity and so was the beauty of my warm face and the loose humanity of my limbs and the racy health of my red rich blood. To leave it all without good reason and to smash the little empire into small fragments was a thing too pitiful even to refuse to think about.&rdquo;</span>
